    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer15036572996232724714">G. K. Chesterton's surreal masterpiece is a psychological thriller that centers on seven anarchists in turn-of-the-century London who call themselves by the names of the days of the week. Chesterton explores the meanings of their disguised identities in what is a fascinating mystery and, ultimately, a spellbinding allegory.<br/><br/>As Jonathan Lethem remarks in his Introduction, Th</span>
  <span id="freeText15036572996232724714" style="display:none">G. K. Chesterton's surreal masterpiece is a psychological thriller that centers on seven anarchists in turn-of-the-century London who call themselves by the names of the days of the week. Chesterton explores the meanings of their disguised identities in what is a fascinating mystery and, ultimately, a spellbinding allegory.<br /><br />As Jonathan Lethem remarks in his Introduction, The real characters are the ideas. Chesterton's nutty agenda is really quite simple: to expose moral relativism and parlor nihilism for the devils he believes them to be. This wouldn't be interesting at all, though, if he didn't also show such passion for giving the devil his due. He animates the forces of chaos and anarchy with every ounce of imaginative verve and rhetorical force in his body.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15036572996232724714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It's definitely deeper. At it's heart of hearts it answers the question "Why does God allow suffering?" set against the backdrop of martyrs for their <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It's definitely deeper. At it's heart of hearts it answers the question "Why does God allow suffering?" set against the backdrop of martyrs for their cause, heroic virtues in the face of absolute terror and a grand chase where nothing is ever as it seems. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140183887" href="/book/show/259053.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=top-shelf">top-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/858949-chris?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14145250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5056817671708962559">I lost my backpack thanks to this book.<br/><br/>It was years and years ago, probably my first winter in Japan, and I'd picked up this book at Maruzen. I had heard about Chesterton, mainly from the dedication page of Pratchett and Gamian's <i>Good Omens</i> ("The authors would like to join the demon Crowley in dedicating this book to the memory of G.K. Chesterton. A man who knew what was going on.") and the title looked weird enough to be entertaining. So, I was reading the book on the train, as I often do, and </span>
  <span id="freeText5056817671708962559" style="display:none">I lost my backpack thanks to this book.<br /><br />It was years and years ago, probably my first winter in Japan, and I'd picked up this book at Maruzen. I had heard about Chesterton, mainly from the dedication page of Pratchett and Gamian's <i>Good Omens</i> ("The authors would like to join the demon Crowley in dedicating this book to the memory of G.K. Chesterton. A man who knew what was going on.") and the title looked weird enough to be entertaining. So, I was reading the book on the train, as I often do, and I had my backpack on the floor between my feet. When the train got to my station, I stood up, still reading, and walked off.<br /><br />It wasn't until I had to put the book down again to eat that I realized I no longer had my backpack.<br /><br />This was no small problem, either - the bag had a lot of important stuff in it, not the least of which was my Palm Pilot with all my friends' addresses on it. There were also about two dozen Christmas cards in there, along with other various and sundry things. And it was a <i>good bag</i>, too.<br /><br />Long story short (too late), I never got the bag back. The staff at my school, and even one of the students, were kind enough to call the Keihan lost &amp; found a few times to see if anyone had turned it in, but with no luck. And whoever got it didn't do the obvious thing and look at the return address on <i>every single one of those Christmas cards, nooo....</i><br /><br />Ahem. I'm over it. Really.<br /><br />My point is this: beware the seductive power of this book. Beware the enchantments laid upon it, and the dreamlike web that it weaves. For if you let it, this book will enrapture you, and gods help you if that happens.<br /><br />The story is one that sucks you in almost from the first page, when two passionate poets argue the worth and detriment of society. Should it be torn down, and let chaos reign in the world? Is order the true glory of humanity, the crowning jewel of mankind? Should the existing paradigm by praised or destroyed, and is he who advocates the path of anarchy true to that path?<br /><br />From that moment, that confrontation of poet-philosophers, we are drawn into a dark heart of true anarchy, where no one can be trusted to be who he appears to be. And not even the protagonist himself can be absolutely sure where his path will end.<br /><br />Needless to say, I think this book was awesome on many levels. The whole thing reads like a dream, moving in and out of locales with odd fluidity, and it's honestly hard to put it down. It has a great cast of characters, each one distinct and interesting and worth your attention, and a great ending that, while not making a whole lot of sense, is entirely fitting.<br /><br />What's really interesting is the modern applicability of this story. Its major theme is that of law versus anarchy, and when Chesterton wrote this back one hundred years ago in 1908 the anarchist movement was seen as a real threat. These people were not the angry kids, spray-painting Anarchy signs all over the place and listening to punk rock. The fringe radicals of the Anarchist movement advocated violence. They liked dynamite and struck terror in the hearts of the citizenry, much in the way that terrorists still do today. And like modern terrorists, they were driven by a twisted and dark ideology which placed their own motivations above society. In the world that Chesterton has made, the Law is in a perpetual battle with the forces of chaos, the dark and shadowy enemies who are always out to destroy us.<br /><br />Sound familiar?<br /><br />The hunt for terrorists is a great plot for any writer, and hundreds of them - good and bad - have used this trope as a way of telling a story. Chesterton, however, reached into the heart of that idea and found the uneasy twist that we are not always willing to deal with. He found the Nietzschean paradox about what happens when you battle monsters, and saw that it could very well be true. He has shown us that it is dangerous to act without knowing the truth, even if the truth isn't what you want it to be.<br /><br />Neil and Terry were right - Chesterton knew what was going on. This book is just as relevant today as it was a century ago, even if Chesterton never meant it to be. No matter what the subtitle to the book may be, and no matter how he may have meant it, the book is still valuable to us. Well worth reading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5056817671708962559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2376535577" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2376535577" />
    <a title="Fergus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus"><img alt="Fergus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567548184p2/70395042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2376535577?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fergus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fergus" href="/user/show/70395042-fergus">Fergus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/70395042-fergus?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2376535577" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7190828101502533137">Gilbert Keith Chesterton’s own life stories were every bit as madcap and zany as this book is. I’ll tell you a bit more, if you like...<br/><br/>One day, during his days of his éminence grise littéraire - the days late in his unbuttoned life of entre deux guerres - we find him on his own madcap mystery tour on the de rigeur readings and signings circuit. The total stress and if-this-is-Friday-it-must-be-Paris kaleidoscopic feeling of it all, must have overwhelmed this poor, usually windbaggish bonhomme...</span>
  <span id="freeText7190828101502533137" style="display:none">Gilbert Keith Chesterton’s own life stories were every bit as madcap and zany as this book is. I’ll tell you a bit more, if you like...<br /><br />One day, during his days of his éminence grise littéraire - the days late in his unbuttoned life of entre deux guerres - we find him on his own madcap mystery tour on the de rigeur readings and signings circuit. The total stress and if-this-is-Friday-it-must-be-Paris kaleidoscopic feeling of it all, must have overwhelmed this poor, usually windbaggish bonhomme...<br /><br />For, totally lost and panic-stricken, he cabled his worry-wart wife tersely:<br /><br />“Am in Golders Green. Where SHOULD I be?”<br /><br />Came the prompt longsuffering reply from his wife:<br /><br />“HOME!”<br /><br />And, oh, Yes - fittingly, THESE are the madcap adventures of a mild-mannered Scotland Yard investigator who has stumbled onto an Anarchist plot in Edwardian London, but can't reveal it to anyone. Art mirrors life.<br /><br />Substitute "terrorist" for "anarchist", substitute "post-Brexit" for "Edwardian" London, and you have the makings of a rollicking good yarn.<br /><br />And Chesterton delivers! <br /><br />Being Catholic, he has an acutely suspicious eye for pure evil - which sobriquet precisely fits this odd and ornery assortment of bad guys. <br /><br />And he expertly holds our attention to the end, a dénouement which is truly apocalyptic - in the best religious sense of the word. <br /><br />It has to be that way, you know! <br /><br />Because, you know, the more our awareness grows, the more evil becomes amorphous. Part of the scenery. But... it’s there.<br /><br />But that doesn’t mean we won’t resist it all the more. But our resistance sustains and feeds evil. And all our seemingly innocuous indulgences give it ample space to grow to a blackly cynical adulthood.<br /><br />C.S. Lewis has noted wisely that evil is by nature parasitic. It grows stronger the more we try to be good. Why do you think good kids are more likely to be bullied?<br /><br />That’s the raison d’être for Apocalypse. It’s like someone calling in the Cosmic Cops. God blows the whistle that ends the game.<br /><br />Whether it’s an earthly one or a fictional type as here, apocalypse is the only possible eschatological answer to evil that has grown out of all proportion.<br /><br />So here Chesterton is faced with that same type of crime - one calling for a Deus ex Machina!<br /><br />Modern times, which were really only beginning when he wrote this, had already blurred the lines between good and evil. So what does he do? He confuses us even more!<br /><br />Pure poetic license.<br /><br />That was one of his own favourite stock-in-trades - blurring the lines between extremes and absolutes. You no longer know which side is Up.<br /><br />He creates such weird and wonderful, baroquely crowded, phantasmagorical stories, all a delightful PARODY of our crazy times.<br /><br />And that’s why the stunning apocalyptic conclusion of this novel WORKS. <br /><br />It is as fantastic as every changing mood, every twist, every bizarre character in this wonderful story.<br /><br />And what is he telling us?<br /><br />That the only result of mass, widespread confusion and anarchy can be Apocalypse. <br /><br />But that can be a peaceful, reassuring mini Apocalypse. As it is here. For in the end, ALL’S WELL THAT ENDS WELL!<br /><br />Suddenly, everything ‘dulce et decorum est.’ The world returns to its Eden...<br /><br />It is no wonder that Chesterton called this yarn a "nightmare"... but it’s a nightmare that's loads of fun, and you know why?<br /><br />It’s all, every last bit of it, JUST a DREAM!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7190828101502533137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4221242490119508806">They say that LSD was first synthesisterised in 1938, so it couldn't be that. But opium was imbibed in British society as we know from Thomas de Quincy up to Sherlock Holmes, so I'm going with opium.<br/><br/>This strange novel is a phantasmagoria which begins as a surrealistic spoof of Boy's-Own detective adventures in which our hero infiltrates the central council of the evil anarchists who are bent on destroying human society. Gathering more absurd elements (elephant chases through central London, medi</span>
  <span id="freeText4221242490119508806" style="display:none">They say that LSD was first synthesisterised in 1938, so it couldn't be that. But opium was imbibed in British society as we know from Thomas de Quincy up to Sherlock Holmes, so I'm going with opium.<br /><br />This strange novel is a phantasmagoria which begins as a surrealistic spoof of Boy's-Own detective adventures in which our hero infiltrates the central council of the evil anarchists who are bent on destroying human society. Gathering more absurd elements (elephant chases through central London, medieval dance raves), it ends up as some kind of incoherent religious parable. The only sense I could make of it was that the message is Hindu - all of the world is divine, all of the world is God, all of the world is God dancing joyously with herself. That's about it, if anyone can nail it down more than that, I'm all ears.<br /><br />As I read this, two things struck me, aside from thinking GK Chesterton's cocoa had been spiked with acid - I thought of an Arthur Penn movie from 1966 called The Chase, which begins conventionally and gets weirder and wilder as it progresses - must see that one again. And I thought that I've never come across so many beards in a single novel - maybe GK was a male facial hair fetishist - every character, and they're ALL men, has their beard or lack of beard carefully noted, so many beards there are that each time I opened my copy I thought I heard sociologists singing folk songs.<br /><br />In one word : bonkers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4221242490119508806" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText4886307362818220809" style="display:none">What…?<br><br>What the hell did I just read?<br><br>Anarchists and poets. That part was deliciously, rebelliously fun to read. <div id="review_34914997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/34914997" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/34914997?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer34914997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1912729675635709309">The Man Who Was Thursday reads like P.G. Wodehouse writing from a Phillip K. Dick plot while on a Nyquil bender. It begins with two poets arguing in the park about whether poetry is more akin to law or anarchy. It turns out that the poet espousing anarchy is actually a member of an anarchist soceity and takes Syme, the other poet, to their meeting place to prove it after a vow of secrecy. Syme is actually a member of an anti-anarchy branch of Scotland Yard and usurps Gregory's spot as the new Th</span>
  <span id="freeText1912729675635709309" style="display:none">The Man Who Was Thursday reads like P.G. Wodehouse writing from a Phillip K. Dick plot while on a Nyquil bender. It begins with two poets arguing in the park about whether poetry is more akin to law or anarchy. It turns out that the poet espousing anarchy is actually a member of an anarchist soceity and takes Syme, the other poet, to their meeting place to prove it after a vow of secrecy. Syme is actually a member of an anti-anarchy branch of Scotland Yard and usurps Gregory's spot as the new Thursday in the Council.<br /><br />Gabriel Symes tells the story of his own recruitment into Scotland Yard by a philosopher policeman and goes on to infiltrate the Council of Days, each one taking the name of a day of the week.<br /><br />None of the Council members are what they seemed at first glance. About halfway through, I was convinced none of them were actually anarchists.<br /><br />I'm a little torn between whether I like this better than The Napoleon of Notting Hill. They probably really shouldn't be compared since they're different kinds of books.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1912729675635709309" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1007376752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1007376752" />
    <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya"><img alt="Leonard Gaya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442984307p2/27098959.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1007376752?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leonard Gaya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leonard Gaya" href="/user/show/27098959-leonard-gaya">Leonard Gaya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22546338-the-man-who-was-thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1007376752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12098508821485631831">Possibly the shortest way to describe Chesterton’s famous novel is to say that it is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24213.Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland___Through_the_Looking_Glass' title='Alice's Adventures in Wonderland & Through the Looking-Glass by Lewis Carroll' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a> for grownups. The story of Gabriel Syme is just as bizarre as that of little Alice. It also echoes in many ways with the oppressing nightmares of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka' title='Franz Kafka' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kafka</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky' title='Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Dostoyevsky</a>.<br/><br/>Still, <i>The Man Who Was Thursday</i> starts like a rather typical detective novel. The protagonist comes in contact with a small group of anarchists/nihilists in a London basement who, to all appearances, are planning a</span>
  <span id="freeText12098508821485631831" style="display:none">Possibly the shortest way to describe Chesterton’s famous novel is to say that it is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24213.Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland___Through_the_Looking_Glass" title="Alice's Adventures in Wonderland &amp; Through the Looking-Glass by Lewis Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a> for grownups. The story of Gabriel Syme is just as bizarre as that of little Alice. It also echoes in many ways with the oppressing nightmares of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5223.Franz_Kafka" title="Franz Kafka" rel="nofollow noopener">Kafka</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Dostoyevsky</a>.<br /><br />Still, <i>The Man Who Was Thursday</i> starts like a rather typical detective novel. The protagonist comes in contact with a small group of anarchists/nihilists in a London basement who, to all appearances, are planning an attack against the tsar of Russia and the French <i>président</i>. Syme manages to get enrolled as one of the group, but we soon find out that he is, in fact, an undercover policeman, with a mission to stop the anarchists. This revelation is just the beginning of a series of “curiouser and curiouser” turn of events, coming thicker and faster, that will send Syme’s (and thereby the reader’s) head spinning into utter bewilderment and paranoia: “Syme had for a flash the sensation that the cosmos had turned exactly upside down, that all trees were growing downwards and that all stars were under his feet”. The end of the novel strings together a carnivalesque chase around London on elephant back and hot-air balloon, and a final revelation of Dantesque proportions.<br /><br />Chesterton was able to capture, with his wonderfully chiselled prose, the atmosphere of London and the zeitgeist in Europe at the turn of the 20th century: communists, nationalists and anarchists on the rise and the menace of a significant terrorist attack — indeed, a couple of years after the publication of this novel, the assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand tipped the world over into war.<br /><br />He also managed to turn a spy mystery into an utterly dizzying reflection on religious symbolism, mysticism and the nature of reality. This way of depicting ordinary existence and then twisting and wringing it out in ways that make it almost unrecognisable, but somehow express something hidden within it, undoubtedly inspired the surrealist movement. And, later on, quite a few American authors as well, like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4764.Philip_K__Dick" title="Philip K. Dick" rel="nofollow noopener">Philip K. Dick</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2778055.Kurt_Vonnegut_Jr_" title="Kurt Vonnegut Jr." rel="nofollow noopener">Kurt Vonnegut</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4462369.William_S__Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs" rel="nofollow noopener">William Burroughs</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/235.Thomas_Pynchon" title="Thomas Pynchon" rel="nofollow noopener">Thomas Pynchon</a>, even <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman" title="Neil Gaiman" rel="nofollow noopener">Neil Gaiman</a>. Not forgetting some Latin-American magic-realist writers, primarily, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/500.Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges" rel="nofollow noopener">Jorge Luis Borges</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12098508821485631831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_728463945" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780881842258" href="/book/show/985376.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer728463945" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17874228394330476543">A sure fire cure for writer’s block.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255883._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Now, my opium-toking friend, you are on the road to writing a classic, time-tested piece of literature that’ll influence writers for decades to come.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255884._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>It’s difficult to give any sort of concrete plot synopsis without major spoilers, but, Gabriel Syme, a police detective recruited by odd means into an anti-terrorist squad, infiltrates a band of seven anarchists all named after the days of the week. Sunday is the leader; Mr. Syme is now Thursday.<br/><br/>Wacky surreal nihi</span>
  <span id="freeText17874228394330476543" style="display:none">A sure fire cure for writer’s block.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255883._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now, my opium-toking friend, you are on the road to writing a classic, time-tested piece of literature that’ll influence writers for decades to come.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255884._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It’s difficult to give any sort of concrete plot synopsis without major spoilers, but, Gabriel Syme, a police detective recruited by odd means into an anti-terrorist squad, infiltrates a band of seven anarchists all named after the days of the week. Sunday is the leader; Mr. Syme is now Thursday.<br><br>Wacky surreal nihilistic hijinks ensue.<br><br>Like many books written around this time, The Great Scout and Cathouse Thursday (Still with me, Goodreader?), attempts to take on a slice of the times, in this case anarchism. Chesterton also throws in an occasional religious epiphany and a WTF-I-don’t-remember-this-from-Sunday-School ending.<br><br>Despite some mind bending elements, this is a nice tongue-in-cheek adventure, loaded with wit and quotable passages – the chapter where the protagonists are pursued by a mob is especially memorable. Reading it, reminded me of a decent episode of the Avengers.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255885.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>This was a buddy read with the Pantsless-opium-smoking-den-of-non-crunchy anarchists.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255886.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>File this one under wacky literary classics that don’t suck but will leave you scratching your head.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1536151496i/26255887._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br></span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1133525216" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1133525216" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1133525216?book_show_action=true">Dec 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781449508869" href="/book/show/7072518-the-man-who-was-thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1133525216" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9057639967676432681"><b>”A man’s brain is a bomb,” he cried out, loosening suddenly his strange passion and striking his own skull with violence. “My brain feels like a bomb, night and day. It must expand! It must expand! A man’s brain must expand, if it breaks up the universe.”</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/e0a5ece4-ff40-49cc-b5ed-bd860cea1a26_zpsm117iexx.png.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579992272i/28841815.png' alt=' photo e0a5ece4-ff40-49cc-b5ed-bd860cea1a26_zpsm117iexx.png' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Gabriel Syme attends a dinner party of his friend, the poet Lucian Gregory. He is there under a pretense of friendship, but his true intention is to find out if his friend can be his entry into joining a group of anarchists. You see, Gabriel</span>
  <span id="freeText9057639967676432681" style="display:none"><b>”A man’s brain is a bomb,” he cried out, loosening suddenly his strange passion and striking his own skull with violence. “My brain feels like a bomb, night and day. It must expand! It must expand! A man’s brain must expand, if it breaks up the universe.”</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/e0a5ece4-ff40-49cc-b5ed-bd860cea1a26_zpsm117iexx.png.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579992272i/28841815.png" alt=" photo e0a5ece4-ff40-49cc-b5ed-bd860cea1a26_zpsm117iexx.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Gabriel Syme attends a dinner party of his friend, the poet Lucian Gregory. He is there under a pretense of friendship, but his true intention is to find out if his friend can be his entry into joining a group of anarchists. You see, Gabriel Syme <i>”was not merely a detective who pretended to be a poet; he was really a poet who had become a detective.”</i> There might be some assumptions that the best way to infiltrate an anarchy group is by hanging out in dive bars, brothels, and dens of inequity (my favorite) where the disgruntled, unwashed masses would gather, but Syme is much more suited to mingling with the intellectual set. These men of high ideals might see anarchy in a romantic light and prove to be as dangerous in their naivete as the man, scarred by life, looking to get even with a government for ill treatment or with a society who chose to ignore him. <br><br><i>”The ordinary detective goes to pot-houses to arrest thieves; we go to artistic tea-parties to detect pessimists.The ordinary detective discovers from a ledger or a diary that a crime has been committed. We discover from a book of sonnets that a crime will be committed. We have to trace the origin of those dreadful thoughts that drive men on at last to intellectual fanaticism and intellectual crime.”</i><br><br>Syme, purposely, pushes his friend. Traps him really, into feeling a need to prove to Syme that he is a true anarchist and not just a man of radical thought incapable of deed. Syme tries to reassure Gregory’s pretty sister that all will be fine. She feels her brother may have said too much. <i>”Now, sometimes a man like your brother really finds a thing he does mean. It may be only a half-truth, quarter-truth, tenth-truth; but then he says more than he means---from sheer force of meaning it.”</i><br><br>I’d like to know how many times I’ve said something that sounds clever, but logically is full of holes. Someone pops off with some dismissive comment, and the next thing I know, I’m scrambling to defend a thought that was barely a concept to begin with. I’m bailing water out of the boat and trying to patch the bottom at the same time, but I’m too stubborn to just let it go because I know the seed of the idea was something worth defending. So we do wonder if Gregory has any real idea of what true anarchy is or is he just a bored poet who finds the whole idea of belonging to a bomb throwing organization... exciting. <br><br>In other words, is he a true believer or an annoying, bombastic, romantic moron?<br><br>For the purposes of our hero Syme, it may not matter. The young man turns out to have a legitimate connection to a group of anarchists who each go by a name of the week. Gregory is intent on becoming Thursday, but Syme convinces the group to add him to their network instead of his friend. He deftly gets what he wants and at the same time puts his friend out of harm's way. <br><br>Syme is a <b>”rebel against rebellion”</b> which is really, if truth be known, what I am as well. I don’t want the general social order to be disrupted. Usually the people who die when a bomb is exploded are just normal, hardworking people who are picking up food for dinner, or dancing with some friends, or going to work. Their deaths are meaningless, except for the fact that their death provides a number that will have terrorists giving each other high fives and politicians wringing their hands. So I’m against anarchy because all it does is destabilize society in an attempt to replace a government with a new government that would quickly resemble the old government. <br><br><b>Besides bombs, gunfire, rape, murder, and all that screaming tends to disrupt my reading time.</b><br><br>G. K. Chesterton was a serious man passionately interested in the occult, theology, and philosophy. Usually when I see those three branches of study all attached to the same individual, I think to myself that this was a person questing to understand the mysteries of life. The interesting thing about this book is you can read it on a multitude of levels and still enjoy the book. You can see it as a metaphysical thriller or as sarcastic political intrigue or as commentary on a society searching for god in all the wrong places. <br><br>The power in the anarchist group rests with the man Sunday, who intimidates the rest of the members. He is a large man or does he just seem to expand when he needs to make a point. His eyes are blue, blue as the sky. His hair is snow white, like the peaks of the highest mountains. As the plot turns fantastical, he takes on a supernatural aspect that leaves this reader wondering if he was the god, or a god, or just a man touched by god. <br><br>Of course, it all becomes comical as one after the other, the members of this anarchist society, turn out to be someone other than what they pretended to be. <br><br>I mentioned philosophy; how about this for something to ponder? <br><br><b>“‘Listen to me,’ cried Syme with extraordinary emphasis.’Shall I tell you the secret of the whole world? It is that we have only known the back of the world. We see everything from behind, and it looks brutal. That is not a tree, but the back of a tree. That is not a cloud, but the back of a cloud. Cannot you see that everything is stooping and hiding a face? If we could only get round in front -.’”</b><br><br>There is also intrigue. Syme is finally relaxing in the belief that he has lost a man who has been tailing him all over the city. <br><br><i>”When he had been seated for about half a minute, he heard behind him a sort of heavy asthmatic breathing. <br><br>Turning sharply, he saw rising gradually higher and higher up the omnibus steps a top hat soiled and dripping with snow, and under the shadow of its brim the short-sighted face and shaking shoulders of Professor de Worms.”</i><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/G.20K.20Chesterton_zpsiaflxwbf.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1579992272i/28841816._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo G.20K.20Chesterton_zpsiaflxwbf.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Chesterton was a large man standing 6’4” and weighing 286 pounds.</b><br><br>There is no doubt in my mind that G. K. Chesterton was brilliant, quite possibly a renaissance man in his desire to understand everything. His prose is at times exquisitely glistening with honey dipped poetry. The book can be confusing with twists and turns made more difficult with an overlay of nightmarish fantasy. I wish I’d been able to read it in one sitting so I could keep the reins of the many divergent thoughts firmly held in my hands like a team of prancing Lipizzan horses. <br><br>This is a fascinating book that deserves to be read more than once, and without a doubt I’d be closer to understanding exactly what Chesterton was intending the more times I read it. My copy of the book will be slid back on the shelf very gently in case there are any bold ideas or a stray piece of dynamite that could roll out on the floor at my feet. Both are equally dangerous, and I’m simply not as fast on my feet as I used to be. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9057639967676432681" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_211545985" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/211545985" />
    <a title="Praj" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3381634-praj"><img alt="Praj" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501486663p2/3381634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/211545985?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Praj" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Praj" href="/user/show/3381634-praj">Praj</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140183887" href="/book/show/259053.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer211545985" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15420731760288816623"><i>‘Humanity crushed once again’. ‘50 dead, 120 injured’. ‘Grave face of terror strikes again’.</i> Familiar headlines scream through the pages of the newspapers each time a bomb goes off annihilating blameless lives. Through teeth gritting resilience, public outcry resonates through the deafened ears of failed intelligence and faith in the state’s law and order hangs by a thin string. As the weeks pass by rapid sketches of the alleged bombers, email links, forensic reports, collected evidence from the</span>
  <span id="freeText15420731760288816623" style="display:none"><i>‘Humanity crushed once again’. ‘50 dead, 120 injured’. ‘Grave face of terror strikes again’.</i> Familiar headlines scream through the pages of the newspapers each time a bomb goes off annihilating blameless lives. Through teeth gritting resilience, public outcry resonates through the deafened ears of failed intelligence and faith in the state’s law and order hangs by a thin string. As the weeks pass by rapid sketches of the alleged bombers, email links, forensic reports, collected evidence from the attacked ground and pictures of rehabilitating victims are splashed across the dailies. If by any chance the investigation comes through, anonymous visages covered with black rags are photographed outside the courtroom, readied for trial procedures, which may go on for months, maybe even years. As the days go by, life returns to normalcy (yes! It is a tricky word); everything is forgotten and the news fade until once again “humanity is crushed” by another dastardly attack. The analytical carnival starts once again. This is the time I dearly wish we had <u>‘philosophical policemen’</u> just like Chesterton describes in his book. Policemen- (officers of law), who would discover the book of sonnets and verses from where the crimes will be committed; those that recognize the intricate web of intellectual crimes. The derivation of dreadful thoughts- the human mind, so malicious and calculating camouflaged within an affluent, composed and erudite exterior. It is that very egocentric brainpower which churns out sadistic alterations from harmless verses and then picks vulnerable actors to craft that design into realism.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“Evil philosopher is not trying to alter things but to annihilate them”.</b>
</i><br />	<br />This book is more than a mere plot of undercover detectives and their clandestine exploration of the Secret anarchist Councilmen. Chesterton pens that a small time criminal is more of a good person. His aim is to eradicated only a certain obstacle and not annihilate the edifice. What caught my eye in one of the chapters was the elucidation of stereotyping poverty to rebellious festering. <br /><br /><i>“You’ve got that eternal idiotic ides that if anarchy came it would come from the poor. Why should it? The poor been rebels, but they have never been anarchists; they have more interest than anyone else in there being some decent government. The poor man really has a stake in the country. The rich man hasn’t; he can go away to New Guinea in a yacht. The poor have sometimes objected to being governed badly; the rich have always objected to being governed at all. Aristocrats are always anarchists; as you can see from the baron’s wars”.</i><br /><br />When a bomber or an active terrorist is caught, he mostly turns out to be from an impoverished background, where his ravenous mind and mislaid faith is manipulated to find refuge in an illusionary godly abode. These are mere actors for crying out loud, chosen by the scheming selfish elements who are coward enough to remain behind the backstage curtains. The affluent as elucidated in this narration are the ones to be feared. They have an abundance of monetary resources, have sheltering capacity in far away lands, if need be and have a mind that concocts the unexpected. Where do you think the enormous funds come for fertilizing terror? I do not want elucidate detailed reports of various pathways of monetary funds wired to definite cults or “charitable” institutions that ultimately fund the immoral actions. But, the currency sure is not a bequest from the poor or some excise complements from our paychecks. The respective courtesy comes from those societal fundamentals that remain unscathed or unfazed by decree. Who do you suppose manages the advanced scientific technologies in various bombing devices? The knowledgeable elite, isn’t it? The erudite or should I say the crème de la crème of religious preachers who instead of spreading peace and equality manipulates vulnerable populace digging their raw wounds every time through words that revolt in their bleeding wounds? I could go on and on, as it angers me to see such naivety among the elements of law and order or purposefully turning a blind eye on the so-called modernists who may be responsible in concocting the ongoing mayhem of lawlessness. Why couldn’t there be some ‘philosophical policemen’ here in India or any place that incessantly plays the role of a powerless victim?<br /><br /><u>Chapter 4- The Tale of the Detective</u> is the deciding chapter that outlines infinitesimal details of who Gabriel Syme really is. Syme sneaks his way into a clandestine council of seven men, each named after a day of the week. Syme becomes the inevitable Thursday though a pact he made with Lucian Gregory ,a poet and a true anarchist. Fear catches with Syme as his path deepens into the sinister world of the other six council men; the President being the most feared of all. Chesterton throws a light on various aspects of fear that thrives within and outside us. We rebel against the only side that corrupts us. What makes a mutineer and destroy the very notion of survival? We try and run from fear and pain, until one eventually catches up and makes us susceptible to uncouth rudiments that shelter our mental nakedness. It is the most treacherous survival, if every time we need proof of familiarity to feel safe. When fear caught up with Syme suffocating his senses, he would feel protected only if a blue card ( a source of identification given to every policemen in England) was shown to him. How vulnerable was Syme to live in a world of treachery and deceit? Makes me think of all the trepidation we feel every time we walk outside our homes or travel; the security checks, the sense of familiarity that we seek in bloodcurdling situations, the proof of safety that we search or reveal; spins a web of utter vulnerability that looms within the safest corners of our thoughts. <u>The Man Who Was Thursday</u> is a treasure that needs to be dug up by reading between the lines of a puzzling narrative to know what Chesterton is really saying.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>“Revolt in its abstract can be revolting. It is like vomiting.”</b>
</i><br /><br />Lastly, if everything leads to God and when nature if dissected reveals the face of God, then should I assume that evil is illusionary? Is malevolence the creation of couple menacing minds? If God means endurance then why is such mutinous extermination carried in God’s name after all?<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15420731760288816623" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4392466221267628732" style="display:none">- Mr Syme?<br /><br />- Yes sir.<br /><br />- You wished to make your report in person.<br /><br />- Yes sir.<br /><br />- Not in writing. This is most irregular.<br /><br />- Yes sir. I had expected that I would be talking to--<br /><br />- The person to whom you are referring no longer works for our organisation.<br /><br />- Yes sir. May I ask--<br /><br />- No, you may not. <br /><br />- Yes sir.<br /><br />- Well?<br /><br />- Ah, yes sir. Ah, I understand very well that my account may seem a little, ah, unusual, but you must take into account that the Czar is still with us and has not been--<br /><br />- The Czar?<br /><br />- Yes sir. He hasn't been exploded. At least to the best of my knowledge.<br /><br />- And why, pray, would the Czar have been "exploded"?<br /><br />- Ah, I'm sorry sir. I was tasked with infiltrating them.<br /><br />- And did you succeed?<br /><br />- Yes sir, I did.<br /><br />- Well, that's something. And who, may I ask, were these desperate men? Foreigners?<br /><br />- Not exactly sir.<br /><br />- Radicals?<br /><br />- Ah, not that either sir. With one exception, they all turned out to be members of the metropolitan police force.<br /><br />- You infiltrated an organisation composed entirely of your own colleagues? But then He hijacked a balloon.<br /><br />- A balloon?!!!<br /><br />- Yes sir. I know it sounds a little far-fetched sir. But as you can see, the Czar is alive and well.<br /><br />- Mr Syme, have you been drinking?<br /><br />- No sir. Well, perhaps a bottle or two of Burgundy. And some Saumur.<br /><br />- Have you done anything else that may have put you in this, shall we say, unusual state?<br /><br />- I stayed up rather late last night reading poetry sir. The Divine Comedy. By Signor Dante. It's jolly good stuff. Sir.<br /><br />- Mr Syme, we are both detectives. We examine the forensic evidence. We use logic and we make deductions.<br /><br />- Yes sir.<br /><br />- Do you consider it possible - I repeat, possible - that you dreamed all this?<br /><br />- Ah, in fact sir--<br /><br />- Yes or no.<br /><br />- Ah, yes sir. I can't rule out the possibility that the whole thing was a nightmare induced by reading Dante after a few bottles of good wine. But--<br /><br />- Thank you Mr Syme, that will be all for now.<br /><br />- But sir--<br /><br />- I said that will be all. Dismissed.</span>

<div id="review_445174533" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/445174533" />
    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445174533?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445174533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18029866444789688481"><b>A buddy reads with my friends <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/25353295-carmen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Carmen</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/12618229-jeff' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jeff</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/6523548-ginger' rel='nofollow noopener'>Ginger</a> - if she ever decides to join.</b><br/><br/><b>The true rating is 2.5 stars.</b><br/><br/>The plot is impossible to describe. All readers agree that this is a psychological thriller. This is the only point commonly agreed on. In any case the books starts with two poets arguing whether poetry should serve the law or anarchy - in other words, a typical first world problem. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431542233i/14845691.jpg' alt='first world problem' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>Very quickly we move onto international conspiracy and after this all the way into bizarre and way bey</span>
  <span id="freeText18029866444789688481" style="display:none"><b>A buddy reads with my friends <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/25353295-carmen" rel="nofollow noopener">Carmen</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/12618229-jeff" rel="nofollow noopener">Jeff</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/show/6523548-ginger" rel="nofollow noopener">Ginger</a> - if she ever decides to join.</b><br><br><b>The true rating is 2.5 stars.</b><br><br>The plot is impossible to describe. All readers agree that this is a psychological thriller. This is the only point commonly agreed on. In any case the books starts with two poets arguing whether poetry should serve the law or anarchy - in other words, a typical first world problem. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1431542233i/14845691.jpg" alt="first world problem" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Very quickly we move onto international conspiracy and after this all the way into bizarre and way beyond. Think Alice in Wonderland written while high on heavy drugs. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535243230i/26199978._SX540_.jpg" alt="Dark Alice in Wonderland" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I always use this book as a proof that at the time it was published the drugs (the mind-altering kind) were freely available from any pharmacy. <br><br>In my humble opinion Agatha Christie was one of the best mystery writers and was simply unrivaled when it came to complicated fair mysteries. It is also my opinion that when it came to international conspiracy / spy mysteries The Dame of Mystery always wrote mediocre stuff at best - like somebody was ghostwriting for her. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535243340i/26199981.jpg" alt="Ghostwriter" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It turns out G.K. Chesterton had exactly the same dubious honor. His Father Brown mysteries were interesting with unusual paradoxes. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535243512i/26199995.jpg" alt="Father Brown" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>His anthology The Man Who Knew Too Much had a fresh approach to a private detective idea. His international conspiracy The Man Who Was Thursday was a failure. <br><br>Please do not get me wrong. The first two thirds of the book were interesting and amusing enough with some slight religious undertones. The last third promptly ended up in bizarre zone with readers' head being beaten with a sledgehammer by the religious allusions (for those that did not get it earlier I guess).<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1535243640i/26199997._SX540_.jpg" alt="Sledgehammer" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So the book is better than 2 stars, but there is no way it is worth 3. I will take the easy way out and declare it to be 2.5 stars: by no means I regret reading this.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18029866444789688481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_158625204" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez"><img alt="Paquita Maria Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594814608p2/2508169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/158625204?book_show_action=true">Apr 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paquita Maria Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paquita Maria Sanchez" href="/user/show/2508169-paquita-maria-sanchez">Paquita Maria Sanchez</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140183887" href="/book/show/259053.The_Man_Who_Was_Thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer15381782712438736973">Boy, this was really good <i>until it wasn't at all anymore</i>. An intriguing story which suddenly turned into some sort of muddled message about patriotism? Capitalism? Christianity? Anarchy? Communism? The soul of all mankind? How redheads are hot and god is fat? Don't know, don't care.<br/><br/>Blah. Skip it.</span>

<div id="review_2501536459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2501536459" />
    <a title="Carmen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen"><img alt="Carmen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412617445p2/25353295.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2501536459?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmen" href="/user/show/25353295-carmen">Carmen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Fans of Kafka, Lewis, Camus, Borges</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Carmen by: </span>
      Non-Crunchy
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=christian">christian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=read-around-the-world-2018">read-around-the-world-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=traditionally-published">traditionally-published</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=he-says">he-says</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/25353295-carmen?shelf=published1908">published1908</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2501536459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2788841638863260122"><i>Through all this ordeal his root horror had been isolation, and there are no words to express the abyss between isolation and having one ally. It may be conceded to the mathematicians that four is twice two. But two is not twice one; two is two thousand times one. That is why, in spite of a hundred disadvantages, the world will always return to monogamy.</i> 45%<br/><br/>Okay, a lot of what I have to say about this book will be spoilers. I am going to hide the spoilers.<br/><br/>First, let's examine what I can say with</span>
  <span id="freeText2788841638863260122" style="display:none"><i>Through all this ordeal his root horror had been isolation, and there are no words to express the abyss between isolation and having one ally. It may be conceded to the mathematicians that four is twice two. But two is not twice one; two is two thousand times one. That is why, in spite of a hundred disadvantages, the world will always return to monogamy.</i> 45%<br /><br />Okay, a lot of what I have to say about this book will be spoilers. I am going to hide the spoilers.<br /><br />First, let's examine what I can say without spoilers.<br /><br />Ostensibly, this is a book about an undercover policeman who infiltrates a group of anarchists. It was published in 1908. Remember when anarchists were a thing? Remember Sacco and Vanzetti?<br /><br />Chesterton is a good author. He is skilled at keeping his reader engaged. I kept chuckling, I kept gasping. I was pretty riveted. Things kind of fall apart at the end, but overall it's a fascinating little novel.<br /><br />Chesterton is also quite funny. This book isn't dry, it's very amusing. I laughed out loud more than once. <br /><br />This book reminded me of:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/127515.Invasion_of_the_Body_Snatchers" title="Invasion of the Body Snatchers by Jack Finney" rel="nofollow noopener">Invasion of the Body Snatchers</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/33574273.A_Wrinkle_in_Time__Time_Quintet___1_" title="A Wrinkle in Time (Time Quintet, #1) by Madeleine L'Engle" rel="nofollow noopener">A Wrinkle in Time</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6324090.Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland__Alice_s_Adventures_in_Wonderland___1_" title="Alice's Adventures in Wonderland (Alice's Adventures in Wonderland, #1) by Lewis Carroll" rel="nofollow noopener">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a><br />And many others, but those stick out.<br /><br />There's a lot of great quips, a lot of great observations about life, and the book moves at a fast and interesting pace.<br /><br />Now let's get to some of the spoilers:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[This is a Christian allegory, much along the lines of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11127.The_Chronicles_of_Narnia__Chronicles_of_Narnia___1_7_" title="The Chronicles of Narnia (Chronicles of Narnia, #1-7) by C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chronicles of Narnia</a>.<br /><br />I need to address some common assertions about this novel.<br /><br />For one thing, a lot of people don't know it is a Christian allegory until the end. It's easy to figure out then, because Chesterton basically starts beating you about the head and neck with a Bible. But if you are someone familiar with the Bible, you will catch on to this book's religious bent early on.<br /><br />I mean, at 18% we have this:<br /><br /><i>"To the priest who says these men are the enemies of religion, to the judge who says these men are the enemies of the law, to the fat parliamentarian who says these men are the enemies of order and public decency, to all these I will reply, 'You are false kings, but you are true prophets. I am come to destroy you, and to fulfill your prophecies.'"</i><br /><br />And of course when I read that, I was instantly like Matthew 5:17:<br />"Think not that I am come to destroy the law, or the prophets: I am not come to destroy, but to fulfill."<br /><br />Then I was like, "Oh, this is some biblical shit." <br /><br />Later, around 43%, Syme is like,<br /><br /><i>"You are right. I am afraid of him. Therefore I swear by God that I will seek out this man whom I fear until I find him, and strike him on the mouth. If heaven were his throne and the earth his footstool, I swear that I would pull him down."</i><br /><br />And I was like, "Oh, it's Matthew 5:33-35."<br /><br />Again, ye have heard that it hath been said by them of old time, Thou shalt not forswear thyself, but shalt perform unto the Lord thine oaths:<br /><br />But I say unto you, Swear not at all; neither by heaven; for it is God's throne:<br /><br />Nor by the earth; for it is his footstool: neither by Jerusalem; for it is the city of the great King. - Matthew<br /><br />NOW. There may be WAY more biblical clues and Easter eggs that I am not picking up. I just happen to be quite familiar with Matthew, and so of course when Chesterton messes around with recreating passages from Matthew I am on it like gravy on biscuits. However, he could be fooling around with a lot more biblical stuff that I'm just not cottoning to. I'm no biblical expert. <br /><br />Of course, this doesn't count the end of the book where Sunday is just straight up quoting the Bible, like, <br /><br /><i>"Can ye drink of the cup that I drink of?"</i><br /><br />So it might not dawn on people that this is a Christian allegory until the very end, but I think that depends on how well you know your Bible. <br /><br />I also went into this cold, stone cold, so I had no idea what the fuck was going on. Of course, if you know it is a Christian allegory going in, you will have a different experience.<br /><br /><br />Another thing I want to address is the idea that Chesterton wrote this as a retelling of the book of Job. I see what you mean by that, but I have my doubts. It just didn't seem quite Job-like IMO. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br />Chesterton sees the enemy as The Rich.<br /><br /><i>"You've got that eternal idiotic idea that if anarchy came it would be from the poor. Why should it? The poor have been rebels, but they have never been anarchists; they have more interest than anyone else in there being some decent government. The poor man really has a stake in the country. The rich man hasn't; he can go away to New Guinea in a yacht. The poor have sometimes objected to being governed badly; the rich have always objected to being governed at all. Aristocrats were always anarchists..."</i> 67%<br /><br />There's more like this sprinkled through the book. Poor people are workers and honest and Christian, the rich are lazy and immoral and bored. We have more to fear from the rich, who don't care about God, government, and their fellow human beings than we do from poor people who are simply trying to live.<br /><br /><br />TL;DR - Quite an amusing and strange little book. Where it ends up may not please you, Chesterton does get a little <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[heavy-handed with the Christianity at the end <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, but I thought it was overall enjoyable and rather charming. It's weird, but Chesterton also has a great sense of humor. <br /><br /><i>Like any man, he was coward enough to fear great force; but he was not quite coward enough to admire it.</i> 32%</span>
  <a data-text-id="2788841638863260122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_185951760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/185951760" />
    <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma"><img alt="PattyMacDotComma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577835191p2/1128612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/185951760?book_show_action=true">Jul 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PattyMacDotComma" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma">PattyMacDotComma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/19065884-the-man-who-was-thursday">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa">aa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=mystery-crime-thriller">mystery-crime-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer185951760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14464884970602865998">2★<br/>Loved the language and loved the beginning. It’s like a mad Monty Python story, but it lost me half way through. And to be fair, the Python crew, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett' title='Terry Pratchett' rel='nofollow noopener'>Terry Pratchett</a> and others may well have been weaned on tales from Chesterton, so perhaps he should get more credit.<br/><br/>The main character, Syme, is a detective who is invited to a secret meeting of anarchists who are preparing to overthrow governments using bombs. He promises Gregory, the man who invited him, not to divulge anything of what he says. Gre</span>
  <span id="freeText14464884970602865998" style="display:none">2★<br />Loved the language and loved the beginning. It’s like a mad Monty Python story, but it lost me half way through. And to be fair, the Python crew, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett" title="Terry Pratchett" rel="nofollow noopener">Terry Pratchett</a> and others may well have been weaned on tales from Chesterton, so perhaps he should get more credit.<br /><br />The main character, Syme, is a detective who is invited to a secret meeting of anarchists who are preparing to overthrow governments using bombs. He promises Gregory, the man who invited him, not to divulge anything of what he says. Gregory, in turn, promises to keep Syme’s police identity secret. Both are champing at the bit to break their promises, but . . . <br /><br />Syme attends a meeting to find the President is called Sunday, and the other members are named after days of the week, with a convenient vacancy for Thursday. He finds himself elected to be Thursday. Now what? Is he expected to bomb someone? Where? How?<br /><br />He finds their next meeting at a very public restaurant breakfast table where they all openly discuss anarchy and laugh loudly. The theory is that they will be taken for fools and disregarded (which seems to be true).<br /><br />In amongst the kind of boys-own action, there is a lot of musing and pondering and observing and pontificating on Life, some of which I quite enjoyed, especially considering this was written over a hundred years ago.<br /><br /><b>“Most of the women were of the kind vaguely called emancipated, and professed some protest against male supremacy. Yet these new women would always pay to a man the extravagant compliment which no ordinary woman ever pays to him, that of listening while he is talking.”<br />. . . <br /><br />“And it is always the humble man who talks too much; the proud man watches himself too closely.”</b><br />. . . <br />I quite like this explanation of the power of monogamy:<br /><br /><b>“. . . there are no words to express the abyss between isolation and having one ally. It may be conceded to the mathematicians that four is twice two. But two is not twice one; two is two thousand times one. That is why, in spite of a hundred disadvantages, the world will always return to monogamy.”</b><br /><br />And I’ll leave you with a last one that could explain today’s politics:<br /><br /><b>“The poor have sometimes objected to being governed badly; the rich have always objected to being governed at all.”</b><br /><br />I enjoyed Syme’s gradual uncovering of the secret society, but eventually, the story wore thin. I’m afraid this doesn’t stand up very well against the many years of fantasy and science fiction that have been written since, but I assume it must have been a cracker of a story in its day.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14464884970602865998" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer36110744" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5229222550871800828">A very original, wonderfully quirky, thought-provoking little book about an English detective who infiltrates a group of anarchists. Part fantasy, part mystery, part philosophical, lots of Christian symbolism that is not apparent until later in the book, but you don't have to be a Christian to enjoy it. There is so much going on here that I will have to reread it at some point.</span>

          </span>
